  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText10822646640272011482" style="display:none">Be careful what you wish for...<br /><br />Once upon a time there was a fairy godmother named Desiderata who had a good heart, a wise head, and poor planning skills—which unfortunately left the Princess Emberella in the care of her <em>other</em> (not quite so good and wise) godmother when DEATH came for Desiderata. So now it's up to Magrat Garlick, Granny Weatherwax, and Nanny Ogg to hop on broomsticks and make for far-distant Genua to ensure the servant girl <em>doesn't</em> marry the Prince.<br /><br />But the road to Genua is bumpy, and along the way the trio of witches encounters the occasional vampire, werewolf, and falling house (well this is a <em>fairy tale</em>, after all). The trouble really begins once these reluctant foster-godmothers arrive in Genua and must outwit their power-hungry counterpart who'll stop at nothing to achieve a proper "happy ending"—even if it means destroying a kingdom.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2320523993" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320523993" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320523993?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=pratchett-terry">pratchett-terry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320523993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7254505869254821066">Watch the abyss behind the seemingly harmonic fairy tales while Magrat Garlick starts earning her reputation as one of the tightest young witches beside Tiffany Aching.<br/><br/>Pratchett uses postmodernism<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmodernism'>https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmod...</a><br/>to deconstruct, satirize, and exaggerate many of the stereotypical and unrealistic tropes of legends, mythology, economics, fairytales, fantasy, political science, and humanities. Subverting the opportunism and uniformity of moral and ethical codexes, witches, nig</span>
  <span id="freeText7254505869254821066" style="display:none">Watch the abyss behind the seemingly harmonic fairy tales while Magrat Garlick starts earning her reputation as one of the tightest young witches beside Tiffany Aching.<br /><br />Pratchett uses postmodernism<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmodernism">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmod...</a><br />to deconstruct, satirize, and exaggerate many of the stereotypical and unrealistic tropes of legends, mythology, economics, fairytales, fantasy, political science, and humanities. Subverting the opportunism and uniformity of moral and ethical codexes, witches, night watches, and wizards are sawing on the wrong, awful, and dishonest foundations of Western culture. No matter what, close to each system has some inherent self destruct or madness buttons, and Pratchett, Monty Python, and some lesser known others laid the cornerstone for groundbreaking, extremely dark new works.<br /><br />The continuation of this process towards the morbid can be seen in the amazing adult animation series of the 21st century, while, in contrast, Pratchett is mainly satirizing and using the formula of changed motivations and personalities to give both laughter and insights.<br /><br />Back to the show: <br /><br />The witches aren´t as directly violent as other characters, they use the soft magic of headologie and altruism to help avoiding disasters or with mundane everyday problems. I would subjectively guess that Pratchett liked his female characters and women in general the most, because he puts most positive, progressive ideas in their minds while all other male characters stay pretty dull, egoistic, or good oldfashioned different stages of insanity.<br /><br />Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg are one of the best friend pairings in the Discworld, a symbiotic relationship, also completely differing from the male friendships, later becoming less important when Tiffany Aching enters the stage. But while the males are primarily fighting, alphaing, posing, and incompetencing around, Granny and Nanny don´t just care for everyone else, but have a long going, emancipated womance.<br /><br />A bit of laughing about tourism is in there too and the all time favorite of marriage politics can never be bad. Oh and, of course, zombies.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/WitchesAbroad">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /><br />This one is added to all Pratchettian reviews:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibenwelt-Romane#Liste_der_Romane">https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibe...</a> <br />The idea of the dissected motifs rocks, highlighting the main real world inspirational elements of fiction and satire is something usually done with so called higher literature, but a much more interesting field in readable literature, as it offers the joy of reading, subtle criticism, and feeling smart all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7254505869254821066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6069095623010606784">Hilarious.<br/><br/>This made me think of the 1988 John Landis film starring Eddie Murphy Coming to America. Not that either story are very much similar at all, except that the most charismatic part of each was the mesmerizing interaction between the characters. Just as Eddie Murphy and Arsenio Hall (who each played multiple characters) were the most enjoyable part of that otherwise mediocre film, so too does the dialogue between Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Og, and Magrat Garlick make this 1991 Pratchett Dis</span>
  <span id="freeText6069095623010606784" style="display:none">Hilarious.<br><br>This made me think of the 1988 John Landis film starring Eddie Murphy Coming to America. Not that either story are very much similar at all, except that the most charismatic part of each was the mesmerizing interaction between the characters. Just as Eddie Murphy and Arsenio Hall (who each played multiple characters) were the most enjoyable part of that otherwise mediocre film, so too does the dialogue between Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Og, and Magrat Garlick make this 1991 Pratchett Discworld novel so much fun.<br><br>In this the twelfth Discworld book, Sir Terry revisits his Witches sub-series that he left in 1988’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_" title="Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Wyrd Sisters</a> and to marvelous effect – a fairy godmother has died and so the three must travel to Genua to settle matters. This presents a vehicle whereby Pratchett, comic genius that he is, to explore and have fun with fairy tales and folklore of all sorts.<br><br>Like the Shrek films (starting in 2001) the idea of a parody of fairy tales is a fecund field of plenty – and Pratchett has seemingly no end of material from which to draw. An observant reader will see abundant use of Grimm, Disney and even L. Frank Baum.<br><br>Like so many of the Discworld books, Pratchett also uses the comic setting of the novel to explore and comment upon various elements of human nature and to administer satire from the playful to the biting.<br><br>An uproarious good time.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1581341865i/28938723._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="294" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552134651" href="/book/show/833427.Witches_Abroad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=read-again">read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=humorous">humorous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22809267-adrian?shelf=fantasy-and-magic">fantasy-and-magic</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2468673539" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1122905352387093676">Review to follow, soon, honest, I promise 😬<br/><br/>Ok so this is the last book I shall read and review this year, and what a way to end the year ( I shall wax more lyrical about my year in books in my "year in books" ha ha).<br/><br/>I first read some Discworld novels back in the early/mid 90s and thoroughly enjoyed them. I often wonder why I didn't read more of them or even continue reading them, but hey ho I didn't, and I suppose I should be glad I somehow (with nudges from GR friends) got back into them.<br/>So rat</span>
  <span id="freeText1122905352387093676" style="display:none">Review to follow, soon, honest, I promise 😬<br /><br />Ok so this is the last book I shall read and review this year, and what a way to end the year ( I shall wax more lyrical about my year in books in my "year in books" ha ha).<br /><br />I first read some Discworld novels back in the early/mid 90s and thoroughly enjoyed them. I often wonder why I didn't read more of them or even continue reading them, but hey ho I didn't, and I suppose I should be glad I somehow (with nudges from GR friends) got back into them.<br />So rather than binge on reading them I very sensibly ( ? ) decided to read them at the rate of one per month, so starting in January, here I am in December reading number 12, and to me <b> this has to be one of the best so far</b>.<br />It is as ever a wonderfully crafted story from Sir Terry, this one focussing on the three witches (Granny Wetherwax, Nanny Ogg and Magrat). Mix in a bad fairy godmother, a number of fairy tales and some voodoo, and you have the makings of a fantastic story. Unlike some of Sir Terry's tales, this is not a parable on modern day life, more a "piss-take" of fairytales and pantomimes in his own inimitable style.<br />My favourite bits of this novel have to be <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Greebo as a human, Magrat as a man eating hussey and of course Granny Weatherwax kicking butt and proving why she is the unofficial senior witch  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />It is to me a solid five stars and may well be my book of December, not my most favourite of the Discworld novels so far, but almost.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1122905352387093676" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2399655593?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25620023-witches-abroad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2399655593" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16678065043912097331">This is the 3rd adventure with my favourite witches starring a colourful cast of characters such as the here introduced Mrs. Gogol as well as Mr. Casanunda (yes, it is who you fear) in addition to the almost always cheerful Nanny, the always grumpy but ultimately "good" Granny and the always dishevelled Magrat. And my favourite cat, of course. <br/><br/>A fairy godmother dies without having organized a successor so she wills Magrat her wand. This duty compells her to seek out her fairy godchild and ensure</span>
  <span id="freeText16678065043912097331" style="display:none">This is the 3rd adventure with my favourite witches starring a colourful cast of characters such as the here introduced Mrs. Gogol as well as Mr. Casanunda (yes, it is who you fear) in addition to the almost always cheerful Nanny, the always grumpy but ultimately "good" Granny and the always dishevelled Magrat. And my favourite cat, of course. <br><br>A fairy godmother dies without having organized a successor so she wills Magrat her wand. This duty compells her to seek out her fairy godchild and ensure her safety. As usual, where Magrat goes, so do Nanny Ogg and Granny Weatherwax. And a good thing, too, because those two sure are the best (though the combination of all three is hilarious as well). They travel "abroad" to Genua where Magrat's fairy godchild lives and discover that wishes are not fishes but something far more dangerous - especially if the <i>other</i> fairy godmother has a soft spot for happily ever afters and is willing to do whatever it takes to enforce one on her fairy godchild (and the entire population of Genua). <br>The three witches travel through some strange places, inspired by tales such as Wizard of Oz (<i>"You know, Greebo, I don't think we're in Lancre"</i>), Baba Yaga, Red Riding Hood, Hansel &amp; Gretel and Sleeping Beauty before "the end game" that is a lot like Cinderella (with nods to Count of Monte Cristo thrown in for good measure) until it is its own thing. And then there is the whole fairy realm / mirror world theme, of course.<br>The merry runaround showcases yet another adversary: the other fairy godmother isn't the only problem because stories <u>want</u> to be told and this one got started so it also wants to play out. Yep, stories, here, are a character in their own right.<br><br>Highlights in this particular book were Greebo with the vampire:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1527146269i/25588663._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Granny playing cards:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1527146269i/25588664.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Greebo being turned into a human (and what he does while being in a man's body):<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1527146269i/25588665._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As is typical for the Discworld and Sir Terry at large, this book not only delivers silly fun and fast-paced action (including not one but TWO witches' duels), but also contemplations on some very deep matters. Nothing as broad and corny as "good vs evil", not exactly, but about wishes and what happens when they are fulfilled, what fairy tales really mean, gender roles, courage vs. cruelty, not losing sight of oneself and more.<br>What makes this so special is how in one second Sir Terry slips in incredibly funny puns such as Nanny's translation skills ("silver plate" which any French-speaker should recognize as the word for "please" or "she had a dire rear" for example), before delivering a mighty punch in the gut such as <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[when the wolf begged Granny to be killed <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. You either cry from laughing too hard or from being drowned in feelings. A feat that not many authors can pull off so brilliantly.<br><br>At first I was a bit sad that this volume was not narrated by Celia Imrie who had read the first Granny Weatherwax book and nailed her voice (though Nigel Planer is brilliant as usual) but the story quickly made me forget about that. In fact, this has instantly become one of my favourite books in this series.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="16678065043912097331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YouKneeK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YouKneeK" href="/user/show/25923230-youkneek">YouKneeK</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1714384792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13089148542231277349">This is the third book in the <i>Witches</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. The last time I enjoyed a <i>Discworld</i> book this much was when I read the second <i>Witches</i> book, <i>Wyrd Sisters</i>. I’m not sure if I’m far enough into <i>Discworld</i> to proclaim my favorite subseries yet, but <i>Witches</i> is the top contender. <br/><br/>As with the previous <i>Witches</i> book, this story focuses primarily on the characters of Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Ogg, and Magrat. Through a series of events, the three are led to journey to a distant country to deal </span>
  <span id="freeText13089148542231277349" style="display:none">This is the third book in the <i>Witches</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. The last time I enjoyed a <i>Discworld</i> book this much was when I read the second <i>Witches</i> book, <i>Wyrd Sisters</i>. I’m not sure if I’m far enough into <i>Discworld</i> to proclaim my favorite subseries yet, but <i>Witches</i> is the top contender. <br /><br />As with the previous <i>Witches</i> book, this story focuses primarily on the characters of Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Ogg, and Magrat. Through a series of events, the three are led to journey to a distant country to deal with an evil fairy godmother who, among other things, is trying to make a girl marry a prince of questionable origin. There are a lot of references to familiar fairy tales, but they’re usually twisted around in an amusing way. I thought the overall plot in this book was far more coherent than that of most of the other <i>Discworld</i> books.<br /><br />Although Magrat can be annoying sometimes, Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg are just hilarious and some of their reactions as they traveled through “foreign parts” made me laugh. One minor complaint I have is that, in <i>Wyrd Sisters</i>, the same characters referred to other places as “forn parts”. They seem to have inexplicably learned how to pronounce “foreign” since then, and for some reason I missed the “forn parts”.<br /><br />Happily, there’s another <i>Witches</i> book only two books away on my <i>Discworld</i> list, so I’ll get the chance to revisit this subseries in the near future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13089148542231277349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1871417499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871417499" />
    <a title="Esme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44110461-esme"><img alt="Esme" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871417499?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esme" href="/user/show/44110461-esme">Esme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=light-fantasy">light-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871417499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16771529323814577048">This is also one of my favorites in all of Discworld, I think Nanny and Granny are at their height in this book as far as their back-and-forth dialogue. The two play so well off of each other in this one it still tickled me even though this must be my 20th re-read of this particular novel.<br/><br/>This book opens with the death of Desiderata Hollow, an older witch who like most witches, had predicted her own demise and knew exactly when she was going to die. This is handy most especially in her case beca</span>
  <span id="freeText16771529323814577048" style="display:none">This is also one of my favorites in all of Discworld, I think Nanny and Granny are at their height in this book as far as their back-and-forth dialogue. The two play so well off of each other in this one it still tickled me even though this must be my 20th re-read of this particular novel.<br /><br />This book opens with the death of Desiderata Hollow, an older witch who like most witches, had predicted her own demise and knew exactly when she was going to die. This is handy most especially in her case because it allowed her to set in motion plans that would continue post-mortem. She made certain, using headology, that Esme, Nanny, and Magrat would make their way to Genua. Esmerelda’s sister, Lilith, has gone mad with power, and Desiderata was no match for her in the end. She’s running the entire city and forcing them into stories of her own wishing, whether or not they wanted to be a part of the story. She’s currently manipulating a young girl into marrying a frog prince, except she’s disguised him to look like a regular prince. The disguise isn’t perfect however, and the young bride to be, Emberella, wants nothing to do with him – she thinks he’s slimy and is concerned by the whispers of the townsfolk about his eyes.<br /><br />Desiderata hooks the three Lancre witches into this by sending Magrat the fairy godmother wand, along with a letter stating that she’s now the second Fairy Godmother to Emberella and that she’s to go to Genua to stop the wedding. (There are always two fairy godmothers, the good one and the bad one of course) So, off go Granny, Nanny and Magrat several thousand miles across the Discworld to Genua, a completely foreign land.<br /><br />On their way to Genua they encounter some dwarves who’ve been trapped in a cave in, Nanny has a house dropped on her, and find a half human half wolf creature that’s in absolute misery and a victim of Lilith’s meddling. Throughout this journey Magrat tries her best to help with her magic wand, but all she can manage to do is turn things into pumpkins. The three witches ate more pumpkin than they ever wanted to on this journey to foreign parts. The letters home that Nanny sends are some of the best parts of this book, the three witches are amazed at things like garlic, and food that hasn’t been boiled.<br /><br />We had some stuff it was chewy you’ll never guess it was snails, and not bad and Esme had three helpins before she found out and then had a Row with the cook and Magrat was sick all night just at the thought of it and had the dire rear. Thinking of you your loving MUM. PS the privies here are DESGUSTING, they have them INDORES, so much for HIGEINE.<br /><br />Once they get to Genua they encounter Mrs. Gogol – a voodoo witch. She’s also displeased with what’s going on in Genua as well, and her magic isn’t to be trifled with. At some point her and Granny both want to “take care of Lilith”, Esme insists it’s a family matter which pushes Mrs. Gogol’s buttons enough for her to perform voodoo on Esme. Oh, and Greebo makes his first appearance as a human. So there’s that 😀<br /><br /><br /> <br /><br />Favorite Quotes:<br /><br />“What was supposed to be so special about a full moon? It was only a big circle of light. And the dark of the moon was only darkness. But halfway between the two, when the moon was between the worlds of light and dark, when even the moon lived on the edge…maybe then a witch could believe in the moon.”<br /><br />“Granny disapproved of magic for domestic purposes, but she was annoyed. She also wanted her tea. She threw a couple of logs into the fireplace and glared at them until they burst into flame out of sheer embarrassment.”<br /><br />“Most people, on waking up, accelerate through a quick panicky pre-consciousness check-up: who am I, where am I, who is he/she, good god, why am I cuddling a policeman’s helmet, what happened last night?<br />And this is because people are riddled by Doubt. It is the engine that drives them through their lives. It is the elastic band in the little model aeroplane of their soul, and they spend their time winding it up until it knots. Early morning is the worst time -there’s that little moment of panic in case you have drifted away in the night and something else has moved in. This never happened to Granny Weatherwax. She went straight from asleep to instant operation on all six cylinders. She never needed to find herself because she always knew who was doing the looking.”<br /><br />“You can’t go around building a better world for people. Only people can build a better world for people. Otherwise it’s just a cage. Besides you don’t build a better world by choppin’ heads off and giving decent girls away to frogs.”<br /><br />Dedicated to all those people – and why not? – who, after the publication of Wyrd Sister, deluged the author with their version of the words of “The Hedgehog Song”. Deary deary me…<br /><br />Audience:<br />comic fantasy<br />witches<br />fairy tales/stories<br />dwarves<br />fairy god mothers<br />short light reads<br /><br /><br />Ratings:<br />Plot: 12.5/15<br />Characters: 15/15<br />World Building: 13.5/15<br />Writing: 15/15<br />Pacing: 14/15<br />Originality: 13.5/15<br />Personal Enjoyment: 10/10<br /><br />Final Score: 93.5/100 – 5 Stars highly recommended</span>
  <a data-text-id="16771529323814577048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2694715472" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2694715472" />
    <a title="Allison Hurd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd"><img alt="Allison Hurd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530670583p2/14495279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2694715472?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allison Hurd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allison Hurd" href="/user/show/14495279-allison-hurd">Allison Hurd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14495279-allison-hurd?shelf=man-author">man-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2694715472" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer476668177453838524">That was fun! I really like the witches and I especially like the rather sweet look at women's companionship. I would love to have a Gytha (let's be honest, I'm going to turn into a Granny Weatherwax well before I can achieve Nanny Ogg) in my golden years.<br/><br/>Check this out, unless you're super sensitive to certain forms of misogyny (like slut-shaming) or the use of voodoo in stories, I have no content warnings! It's about as un-objectionable as you can get in a book with more than 5 sentences in it</span>
  <span id="freeText476668177453838524" style="display:none">That was fun! I really like the witches and I especially like the rather sweet look at women's companionship. I would love to have a Gytha (let's be honest, I'm going to turn into a Granny Weatherwax well before I can achieve Nanny Ogg) in my golden years.<br /><br />Check this out, unless you're super sensitive to certain forms of misogyny (like slut-shaming) or the use of voodoo in stories, I have no content warnings! It's about as un-objectionable as you can get in a book with more than 5 sentences in it!<br /><br /><u>Things to love:</u><br /><br />-<b>The humor</b>. I think he's really honed in on what works by now. There's wit, pratfalls, puns, silliness, sex jokes and all the other types of humor you could list, but none that felt especially forced. It was lovingly ridiculous and so fun.<br /><br />-<b>The concept</b>. Actually a really, really good job talking about story creations and ur-myths! I love how they all interwove, and how he purposefully kicked open the door for non-European myths, too. I'm not sure he did the best job in the world with Afro-Caribbean mythology, but he did a lot better than many current, "woke" settings I've seen done so that's something.<br /><br />-<b>The characters</b>. I just defy you not to giggle along with Nanny, Granny, Magrat and the people they meet. They're such caricatures you instantly identify with them. This is your gran. That's your memere. And they've just gone on holiday.<br /><br />-<b>The world</b>. As ever I'm amazed at how many things Pratchett packed into his world and how well thought out it is. For a series that doesn't take itself very seriously, like the consummate comedian, the author put a loooot of work into the construction of his jokes, this one replete with mountains, bogs, magic and zombies.<br /><br /><u>Things I did not love:</u><br /><br />I don't really have anything to detract from it, other than nitpicks about being careful in representation. While everyone is entitled to disagree, I was pretty impressed with even those sensitive waters. The people of color are human, they have flaws, motives, and redeeming qualities, the white characters respect them, and the author takes time to remind us of the sorts of people not usually found in our stories. Brilliant stuff, I thought, actually.<br /><br />The "missing" star is that while it's super clever and I think did what it intended to do, I have no heartfelt connection. I really, really liked it. I'm not in love, though. A fun installment in a great subseries, of which I will definitely be reading more!</span>
  <a data-text-id="476668177453838524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4871063511532438920" style="display:none"><i>‘We’re her godmothers,’ said Granny.<br />‘That’s right,’ said Nanny Ogg.<br />‘We’ve got a wand, too,’ said Magrat.<br />‘But you hate godmothers, Mistress Weatherwax,’ said Mrs Gogol.<br />‘We’re the other kind,’ said Granny. ‘We’re the kind that gives people what they know they really need, not what we think they ought to want.’</i><br /><br />The witches - Granny, Nanny, and Magrat - are being sent on a mission: the ward of Desidarata needs help to free herself from the malevolent influence of a mysterious force - it's to do with mirrors and frogs and glas slippers, and zombies.<br /> <br />None of the witches have ever been abroad, and as they make their way across the Discworld, mayham lies in their wake, but what is worse....some people have no respect for witches!<br /> <i><br />‘They treated us as if we was ordinary people,’ said Granny, in a shocked voice.<br /> </i><br />Witches Abroad was a lot of fun but there was something missing for me in this one. <div id="review_85717179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85717179" />
    <a title="Steven Harbin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin"><img alt="Steven Harbin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307406902p2/1283483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/85717179?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Harbin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Harbin" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin">Steven Harbin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/25620023-witches-abroad">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85717179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3074245347029297399">I've completed 6 Discworld titles so far (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34494.The_Wee_Free_Men__Discworld___30__Tiffany_Aching___1_' title='The Wee Free Men (Discworld, #30; Tiffany Aching, #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wee Free Men</a>,<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34497.The_Color_of_Magic__Discworld___1__Rincewind___1_' title='The Color of Magic (Discworld, #1; Rincewind, #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Color of Magic</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites__Discworld___3__Witches___1_' title='Equal Rites (Discworld, #3; Witches, #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Equal Rites</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/386372.Mort__Death___1__Discworld___4_' title='Mort (Death, #1; Discworld, #4) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mort</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34499.Sourcery__Discworld___5__Rincewind__3_' title='Sourcery (Discworld, #5; Rincewind #3) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sourcery</a>,<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_' title='Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wyrd Sisters</a>) and I have to say this is my very favorite one thus far. I love the way <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett' title='Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Terry Pratchett</a> takes things that most of us can relate to, such as traveling abroad, and turns them into hilarious and at the same time fantastic Discworld adventures. The three Lancre witches Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Ogg, and Magrat are some of my favorite characters in the whole series, not to mention N</span>
  <span id="freeText3074245347029297399" style="display:none">I've completed 6 Discworld titles so far (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34494.The_Wee_Free_Men__Discworld___30__Tiffany_Aching___1_" title="The Wee Free Men (Discworld, #30; Tiffany Aching, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a>,<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34497.The_Color_of_Magic__Discworld___1__Rincewind___1_" title="The Color of Magic (Discworld, #1; Rincewind, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Color of Magic</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites__Discworld___3__Witches___1_" title="Equal Rites (Discworld, #3; Witches, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Equal Rites</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/386372.Mort__Death___1__Discworld___4_" title="Mort (Death, #1; Discworld, #4) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Mort</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34499.Sourcery__Discworld___5__Rincewind__3_" title="Sourcery (Discworld, #5; Rincewind #3) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Sourcery</a>,<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_" title="Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Wyrd Sisters</a>) and I have to say this is my very favorite one thus far. I love the way <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett</a> takes things that most of us can relate to, such as traveling abroad, and turns them into hilarious and at the same time fantastic Discworld adventures. The three Lancre witches Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Ogg, and Magrat are some of my favorite characters in the whole series, not to mention Nanny's cat Greebo (who reminds me of some Roundworld cats that I have known).<br /><br />This volume starts off with Magrat being willed the wand (and duties) of a Fairy Godmother. She (along with Granny and Nanny) have to go to a foreign land to help a princess NOT marry a prince. I'll never view the fairy tales of my youth the same way again after reading this one. Defintely worth 5 stars. It's not necessary to have read any of the other books but I think that having read at least <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_" title="Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Wyrd Sisters</a> and possibly <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites__Discworld___3__Witches___1_" title="Equal Rites (Discworld, #3; Witches, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Equal Rites</a> helps establish a good understanding of the witches and their motivations and characters. However, once again I don't think it lessens the enjoyment of this book if one hasn't read any of the other novels. It stands alone quite well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3074245347029297399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5454475-toby?shelf=fantastical">fantastical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168812752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer609211112597035771">Witches are abroad and they're sending the Disc's first postcards! Book twelve in the series and Pratchett turns his Discworld funhouse mirror on the matter of destiny and the nature of storytelling, and specifically the nature of fairy tales. The Disc's magic field does strange things with what we humans consider commonplace and every day ideas, so when the fanciful Disneylike idea of a Fairy Godmother germinates in the fertile grounds of Genua all bets are off! But then there isn't much that c</span>
  <span id="freeText609211112597035771" style="display:none">Witches are abroad and they're sending the Disc's first postcards! Book twelve in the series and Pratchett turns his Discworld funhouse mirror on the matter of destiny and the nature of storytelling, and specifically the nature of fairy tales. The Disc's magic field does strange things with what we humans consider commonplace and every day ideas, so when the fanciful Disneylike idea of a Fairy Godmother germinates in the fertile grounds of Genua all bets are off! But then there isn't much that can stand up to the glare of Granny Weatherwax and her unflinchingly rigid way of looking a what is right and more importantly what isn't.<br /><br />The road trip around "Europe" is a mine of wonderfully playful anecdotes featuring the Disc's favourite coven of witches, the complex build up to and the final showdown with the Fairy Godmother a delight but even so it feels slight, like it was a clever idea for a short story expanded upon to the point where it could legitimately be published as novel but never really going further in the way the best of the Pratchett books will. It's good but it's not great as they say.</span>
  <a data-text-id="609211112597035771" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3547284391" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3547284391" />
    <a title="Marc *Dark Reader of the Woods*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29163734-marc-dark-reader-of-the-woods"><img alt="Marc *Dark Reader of the Woods*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602607922p2/29163734.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3547284391?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc *Dark Reader of the Woods*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc *Dark Reader of the Woods*" href="/user/show/29163734-marc-dark-reader-of-the-woods">Marc *Dark Reader of the Woods*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451452252" href="/book/show/513535.Witches_Abroad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29163734-marc-dark-reader-of-the-woods?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3547284391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15481589168776718201">As much as I love the Discworld series, I have to admit that there are several sub-standard entries early in its life (and by "early" I mean "the first 10 years") and I often think that the series might not have survived the publishing world today. Dare I say, new books might have come out too frequently, with two per year for most of the series' lifespan. <i>Witches Abroad</i> is one of those entries that would have benefitted from another revision passe or two before publication. <br/><br/>The main characters </span>
  <span id="freeText15481589168776718201" style="display:none">As much as I love the Discworld series, I have to admit that there are several sub-standard entries early in its life (and by "early" I mean "the first 10 years") and I often think that the series might not have survived the publishing world today. Dare I say, new books might have come out too frequently, with two per year for most of the series' lifespan. <i>Witches Abroad</i> is one of those entries that would have benefitted from another revision passe or two before publication. <br /><br />The main characters (Granny Weatherwax, Nanny Ogg, and Magrat Garlick) are as great as ever and carry the book. Although they do not undergo any significant development, it is always a joy to read their interactions with each other and with hapless outsiders. Other characters are only there as fodder for them to act against. <br /><br />The plot progress and pacing is choppy in the latter half of the book, and this is where the book as a whole breaks down, although not as badly as in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34499.Sourcery__Discworld___5__Rincewind__3_" title="Sourcery (Discworld, #5; Rincewind #3) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Sourcery</a> which is the most egregious example of this intermittent problem in the series. Any Discworld fan knows not to expect anything as mundane as "chapters", but this book might have benefitted from having clearer separation between the main sections of the book. The setup involves re-establishing the status of the witches in their home region of the Ramtop mountains and setting the plot into motion with the death of witch/fairy godmother Desiderata, who passes along her wand and charge. The next part sees our trio of witches traveling to "furrin places" and we enjoy seeing them react to these new environments, or more accurately how they enforce themselves in new places. <br /><br />The final section, beginning with their arrival in their final destination of Genua, is where it largely falls apart. At this point the plot ceases flowing naturally; the supposed plot payoffs were not adequately set up; new elements (the swamp, Mr. Samedi, Mrs. Gogol, most of the enemy players) were inadequately built up. The whole theme of stories fell flat. One intended major character revelation was spoiled a long time earlier to anyone with a passing knowledge of French. <br /><br />In total, the attempt to use the witches as a framework for a Discworld take on fairy tales, like was done with Shakespeare in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_" title="Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Wyrd Sisters</a>, was well-intentioned, approached success at one point, but ultimately didn't pan out. It's too bad that Pratchett didn't spend a little more time with this story, smoothing it out and adding the additional pages it needed to work. It's forgivable, and I still love reading the witches stories, with another coming up just two books later (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34529.Lords_and_Ladies__Discworld___14__Witches__4_" title="Lords and Ladies (Discworld, #14; Witches #4) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Lords and Ladies</a>) in publication order.<br /><br />FINAL WORD: Nanny Ogg's tomcat Greebo has a feature role in this book. I am extremely uncomfortable with the description of him as "rapey" with the intent of humour. If he were merely threatening to all creatures he comes across and an ardent mater with female cats, that would be sufficient, but the text specifically describes him as a rapist of animals, not necessarily restricted to his own species.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15481589168776718201" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_100034214" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100034214" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1343864-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597876967p2/1343864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100034214?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/1343864-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780061809729" href="/book/show/10299436-witches-abroad">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=ridiculously-prolific-authors">ridiculously-prolific-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=anglophilia">anglophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1343864-ashley?shelf=fairy-tales">fairy-tales</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100034214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9891922966197170894"><i>Witches Abroad</i> is the 12th Discworld novel by Sir Terry Pratchett. The thing about Discworld is that you don't necessarily have to read his books in order. They all take place in the same world, and all the characters pop up and make cameos in each other's stories, but it doesn't really matter. But of course, along with having that thing where I can't quit things, I also have that thing where I have to read things in order. One would think being at #12 would be an accomplishment, and one would n</span>
  <span id="freeText9891922966197170894" style="display:none"><i>Witches Abroad</i> is the 12th Discworld novel by Sir Terry Pratchett. The thing about Discworld is that you don't necessarily have to read his books in order. They all take place in the same world, and all the characters pop up and make cameos in each other's stories, but it doesn't really matter. But of course, along with having that thing where I can't quit things, I also have that thing where I have to read things in order. One would think being at #12 would be an accomplishment, and one would normally be right, but Sir "I am a prolific genius" Pratchett has 39 books in his wacky, absurd fantasy world, a world that takes place on a planet that is flat (really literally flat), and which "travels through space on the back of four elephants, which themselves stand on the shell of Great A'Tuin, the sky turtle." And I have 27 books (and counting*) to go.<br /><br />*<i>Despite having been diagnosed with early onset Alzheimer's in 2007, he continues to faithfully churn out (well-received) books.</i><br /><br /><i>Witches Abroad</i> features the fan-favorite set of characters The Witches, and while they're not as funny as the Night Watch, say, they still make me laugh quite a lot. The entire novel is basically a deconstruction of fairy-tales. Three witches have to prevent the princess from marrying the prince, and defeat the fairy-godmother, preventing stories from taking over the world -- and they're the good guys. First there's Granny Weatherwax, a formidable witch who is more akin to a force of nature than an old woman; Nanny Ogg, who is kind of horny; and Magrat Garlick, who is often described as being a "wet hen."<br /><br />The pacing is typical Pratchett. There are no chapters, and the characters are thrown headlong into the action. Like many Discworld novels, <i>Witches Abroad</i> is a wacky landslide of inappropriate humor and clever words that gradually spirals into genuine emotion. It was a bit slow to start, and it didn't get quite real enough at the end, but otherwise, very enjoyable read. Pratchett is good at symmetry, and fittingly enough for a story about the power of stories**, he understands how to work them.<br /><br />**<i>This book is also about mirrors and sisters and cats and zombies, and there are some dwarves and other assorted fairy-tales to be found as well. There's even a Gollum-parody near the beginning.</i><br /><br />And now, as is traditional with a Discworld book review, here are wacky quotes:<br /><blockquote>"Nanny Ogg [...] had a tendency to come out with what Magrat thought of as double-intenders, although in Nanny Ogg's case they were generally single entendres, and proud of it."<br /><br />"Despite many threats, Granny Weatherwax had never turned anyone into a frog. The way she saw it, there was a technically less cruel but cheaper and much more satisfying thing you could do. You could leave them human and make them think they were a frog, which also provided much innocent entertainment for passers-by."<br /><br />"Racism was not a problem on the Discworld, because -- what with trolls and dwarfs and so on -- speciesism was more interesting. Black and white lived in perfect harmony and ganged up on green."<br /><br />"'Baths is unhygienic,' Granny declared. 'You know I've never agreed with baths. Sittin' around in your own dirt like that.'"<br /><br />"Magrat was annoyed. She was also frightened, which made her even more annoyed. It was hard for people when Magrat was annoyed. It was like being attacked by damp tissue."<br /><br />"She hated everything that predestined people, that fooled them, that made them slightly less than human."<br /><br />"You can't go around building a better world for people. Only people can build a better world for people. Otherwise it's just a cage."</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="9891922966197170894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1259302118" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1259302118" />
    <a title="Lindsay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay"><img alt="Lindsay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588746288p2/10326608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259302118?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/10326608-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1259302118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9709597426599963054">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads' rel='nofollow noopener'>SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group. This is an unusual book in this series for me as it was released at a very busy time in my life and I didn't read it as a new release, and in fact, I didn't read it until well after at least the next book in the Witches series came out.<br/><br/>The old witch Desiderata Hollow dies, leaving her role as one of a pair of fairy godmothers to Magrat Garlick and a job for all three of Magrat, Nanny Ogg and Granny Weatherwax in the far-off country</span>
  <span id="freeText9709597426599963054" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads</a> group. This is an unusual book in this series for me as it was released at a very busy time in my life and I didn't read it as a new release, and in fact, I didn't read it until well after at least the next book in the Witches series came out.<br /><br />The old witch Desiderata Hollow dies, leaving her role as one of a pair of fairy godmothers to Magrat Garlick and a job for all three of Magrat, Nanny Ogg and Granny Weatherwax in the far-off country of Genua. The other fairy godmother is in Genua and uses the power of Stories to her own ends. Our witches need to go there and stop the marriage of a young girl to the handsome prince, which is very difficult when the story wants to be told.<br /><br />This book does much to bed down the wonderful characters of the witches and set the foundation for the ongoing series. It's also another outing for the author's ideas about stories as things of their own (a bit different from how <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10__Industrial_Revolution___1_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10; Industrial Revolution, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> handled it and this is actually expanded on well in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64225.The_Science_of_Discworld_II_The_Globe__The_Science_of_Discworld___2_" title="The Science of Discworld II The Globe (The Science of Discworld, #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Science of Discworld II: The Globe</a> where the authors talk about <i>home narrativium</i>.)<br /><br />There's a lot of tongue-in-cheek commentary on parochial tourists venturing into the wider world as well that could have been mean but actually comes across with the author's clear affection for these characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9709597426599963054" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="❀⊱RoryReads⊰❀" class="user" itemprop="url" name="❀⊱RoryReads⊰❀" href="/user/show/25852225-roryreads">❀⊱RoryReads⊰❀</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2154210949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3411885149294189853">4 Stars.<br/><br/>This is the third Witches book in Pratchett's Discworld series and delivers everything you would expect from a Discworld story; adventure, malapropisms galore, double entendres, a general air of silliness and an underlying lesson about the nature of life.<br/><br/>Granny Weatherwax is my favorite Discworld character, but Nanny Ogg is now a very close second with her ribald jokes and songs and her ability to hold her liquor. I've also become rather fond of Magrat, wet hen though she is. If you have</span>
  <span id="freeText3411885149294189853" style="display:none">4 Stars.<br /><br />This is the third Witches book in Pratchett's Discworld series and delivers everything you would expect from a Discworld story; adventure, malapropisms galore, double entendres, a general air of silliness and an underlying lesson about the nature of life.<br /><br />Granny Weatherwax is my favorite Discworld character, but Nanny Ogg is now a very close second with her ribald jokes and songs and her ability to hold her liquor. I've also become rather fond of Magrat, wet hen though she is. If you haven't spent any time with these ladies, I recommend you do so once. They're the perfect antidote to the often soul sucking banality of real life.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1941710117433434246">DNF at 50%<br/><br/>This book is just childish and nonsensical. It's about three witches who travel 'abroad' and run into various fairy tale stories along the way like Sleeping Beauty, Little Red Riding Hood, Wizard of Oz, etc. The witches are ridiculous and more than a little stupid, the plot is lazy, the writing is hard to follow. I wanted to finish but honestly it's not worth my time.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1058284794" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1058284794" />
    <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5252054-irena"><img alt="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425497470p2/5252054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1058284794?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" href="/user/show/5252054-irena">ᴥ Irena ᴥ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=comic-fantasy">comic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=witches">witches</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1058284794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16618075363674139526"><b>4.5</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>'This is a story about stories. Or what it really means to be a fairy godmother. But it’s also, particularly, about reflections and mirrors.'</b>
</blockquote> When Desirata Hollow dies, she leaves her wand to Magrat Garlick. With it, Magrat inherits an obligation to help Princess Emberella <i>not</i> to marry a prince. What Magrat doesn't know is that the princess has two godmothers, one good and one bad. Another important thing in Desirata's will is whatever happens, Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg must not hel</span>
  <span id="freeText16618075363674139526" style="display:none"><b>4.5</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>'This is a story about stories. Or what it really means to be a fairy godmother. But it’s also, particularly, about reflections and mirrors.'</b>
</blockquote> When Desirata Hollow dies, she leaves her wand to Magrat Garlick. With it, Magrat inherits an obligation to help Princess Emberella <i>not</i> to marry a prince. What Magrat doesn't know is that the princess has two godmothers, one good and one bad. Another important thing in Desirata's will is whatever happens, Granny Weatherwax and Nanny Ogg must not help her. We all know what 'mustn't' does when it comes to those two.<br>The three witches and one cat set out to help the poor princess. Their destination is Genua.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1415918029i/11865246._SX540_.jpg" width="380" height="300" alt="Witches Abroad" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The first part of the book describes their journey. Along the way they notice a lot of weird things - creatures from different fairy tales roaming the country. Granny wouldn't be Granny if she didn't fix everything. Only Terry Pratchett could find a way to convince you to be on the opposite side to the one wanting <i>a happy ending</i>. Some of the scenes in this book broke my heart, but most are hilarious. Here you'll find different take on a lot of familiar stories. There is even voodoo. With Granny Weatherwax around every story gets a special treatment and her own brand of ending. <br><br>I loved everything from the fairy tales (each with a new spin) and their constant bickering to the choice of the villain. Even Greebo the cat is awesome. But what I loved the most is Granny, her reactions to the things they encountered. My favourite, the saddest is the moment when Granny meets the wolf. <br><br>If you can't stand a lot of bickering, three different witches being their own weird, funny and occasionally obnoxious selves, completely messed up fairy tales, then I suggest you skip this one. <br><br>The Death makes an appearance too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16618075363674139526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Audrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Audrey" href="/user/show/5630113-audrey">Audrey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2806039528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7245695496512986484">3.5 stars<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506375023ra/24010466.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I believe this is the third book to feature Granny Weatherwax. The previous books are not necessary to understand this one; they just introduce the characters. Some Discworld books are more plot-based and some are more of an excuse to fit in a bunch of jokes. This one is more plot-based.<br/><br/>In this one, Granny, Magrat, and Nanny take on a fairy godmother nemesis. This involves traveling to foreign parts and encountering various fairy tales. They travel through countries that vaguely resembl</span>
  <span id="freeText7245695496512986484" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506375023ra/24010466.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I believe this is the third book to feature Granny Weatherwax. The previous books are not necessary to understand this one; they just introduce the characters. Some Discworld books are more plot-based and some are more of an excuse to fit in a bunch of jokes. This one is more plot-based.<br><br>In this one, Granny, Magrat, and Nanny take on a fairy godmother nemesis. This involves traveling to foreign parts and encountering various fairy tales. They travel through countries that vaguely resemble France, Spain, Transylvania, and New Orleans. The culture clashes are quite amusing.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1580341041ra/28867940.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Death has a small appearance, and Greebo the cat was endearing despite being an a–hole.<br><br>Some parts were funny … <br><i>She had buried at least three husbands, and two of them were already dead.</i><br><br>and others more profound … <br><i>You can’t go around building a better world for people. Only people can build a better world for people. Otherwise it’s just a cage.</i><br><br><a href="https://audreytheeditor.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener"> Book Blog</a><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="7245695496512986484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_566443098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/566443098" />
    <a title="Nathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5238423-nathan"><img alt="Nathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352775557p2/5238423.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/566443098?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan" href="/user/show/5238423-nathan">Nathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=rated-4-star">rated-4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=high-fantasy">high-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5238423-nathan?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer566443098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8599505787762078254"><a href='http://fantasyreviewbarn.blogspot.com//2012/11/the-complete-diskworld-re-read.html#more' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Complete Discworld Reread</a><br/><br/>First things first, I must say my reading comprehension has gone up by quite a lot since I started reviewing all the books I read. For instance I know I have read ‘Witches Abroad’ a half dozen times in my life and never once realized that the ‘Cinderella’ of the story (Emberella) was of mixed heritage. In no way does this affect the story or the review, just jumped out at me for the first time.<br/><br/>Anyway….<br/><br/>Another book following Granny Weatherwax so you must know I am all</span>
  <span id="freeText8599505787762078254" style="display:none"><a href="http://fantasyreviewbarn.blogspot.com//2012/11/the-complete-diskworld-re-read.html#more" rel="nofollow noopener">The Complete Discworld Reread</a><br /><br />First things first, I must say my reading comprehension has gone up by quite a lot since I started reviewing all the books I read. For instance I know I have read ‘Witches Abroad’ a half dozen times in my life and never once realized that the ‘Cinderella’ of the story (Emberella) was of mixed heritage. In no way does this affect the story or the review, just jumped out at me for the first time.<br /><br />Anyway….<br /><br />Another book following Granny Weatherwax so you must know I am all in. Continuing, and expanding on, the theme of stories gaining a life of their own, this time following fairy tales rather than Shakespeare. Magrat inherits a magic wand and the duties of fairy godmother, as well as a long time battle with another fairy godmother. She learns she needs to travel to Genua to stop the serving girl from marrying the prince. The late godmother lets Magrat know she is not to bring Nanny and Granny along, so of course all three witches head to Genua. Off to Genua! And Magrat may have a chance to stop a story if she can get the wand set to something other than pumpkin. <br /><br />A bit different from the last few Discworld books in that it doesn’t have competing stories fighting for space. Instead it is firmly focused on the Witches journey and Emberella ’s fate. This should make it one of the more focused of the series, but I found it to be a bit uneven at first. The traveling especially was hit and miss; providing some of the funniest portions of the story but dragging after a while. Granny continues to be my hero when she takes on the card sharks in riverboat gambling (humming off tune and itching the inside of her ear the whole time). Along the route they see the results of a godmother giving happy endings, with horrifying results (think Shrek 2). <br /><br />Once in Genua the story tightens up. A perfect town is kept that way with an iron first (think Shrek 1. Wow, they had to have read Pratchett before writing those movies). A practitioner of Voodoo has been holding the Fairy Godmother at bay but will need the Lancre witches to tip the balance. Because if the resident fairy godmother has her way Emberella WILL marry the prince and there WILL be happily ever after, whether anyone wants it or not. Both sides know they must now play within the story and each must figure out the best way to cheat within the framework. Who is good and who is bad? Is forced happiness real happiness? Is Granny always the good one, and if so, does she really want to be? <br /><br />Oh yes, some of my favorite parts for others to compare with their own: A small town deciding to stop the running of the bulls after the witches visit, Granny showing a voodoo doll can work both ways, and Magrat proving to be more mongoose than mouse.<br /><br />I can’t be objective because the Lancre witches continue to be my favorite, and have been for years. I also am fond of this book because it had one of Pratchett’s strongest endings, especially withen the sometimes inconsistent early books. The only downside I see at all is the travelling, while funny, dragged a bit too long for my taste.<br /><br />One last interesting fact, pointed out to me before I started rereading this book by another fan; all five major characters in this book are women, and the only male who really matters to the story is actually a cat. Who else but Pratchett could do this so subtle that most wouldn’t even notice? <br /><br />4 stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8599505787762078254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
